For compendious recent surveys of the entire Continental philosophical tradition beginning with Kant and German idealism, see 
Simon Critchley and William Schroeder (eds), [3mA Companion to Continental Philosophy [0m(Blackwell, Oxford, 1998) and Simon Glendinning 
(ed.), [3mThe Edinburgh Encyclopedia of Continental Philosophy [0m(Edinburgh University Press, Edinburgh, 1999). I have expanded 
material from the Introduction to the Blackwellโs Companion in drafting this book. Chapter 7 of this book appeared in a different 
form in the [3mTimes Higher Education Supplement [0m, 6 February 1998, under the title โDare to Thinkโ. For helpful single-volume 
summaries of the Continental tradition, see Robert Solomon, [3mContinental Philosophy Since 1750 [0m(Oxford University Press, Oxford, 
1988) and David West, [3mAn Introduction to Continental Philosophy [0m(Polity Press, Cambridge, 1996). For anthologies containing 
extracts from primary texts, see Richard Kearney and Mara Rainwater (eds), [3mThe Continental Philosophy Reader [0m(Routledge, London, 
1996) and Karen Feldman and William McNeill (eds), [3mContinental Philosophy: An Anthology [0m(Blackwell, Oxford, 1997). 

The argument of Chapter 1 was suggested to me by three books: Pierre Hadot, [3mPhilosophy as a Way of Life [0m(Blackwell, Oxford, 1995), 
Stephen Toulmin, [3mCosmopolis [0m(University of Chicago Press, Chicago, Ill., 1990), and John Cottingham, [3mPhilosophy and the Good Life [0m
(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1998). Also, on the question of the relation of science to the meaning of life, 
Dostoevskyโs [3mNotes from Underground [0mwas frequently on my mind, a text which is a better introduction to philosophy than most. 

The argument of Chapter 2 was strongly influenced by Michael Dummettโs [3mOrigins of Analytical Philosophy [0m(Duckworth, London, 1993) 
and Frederick Beiserโs [3mThe Fate of Reason: German Philosophy from Kant to Fichte [0m(Harvard University Press, Cambridge, Mass., 
1987). For an overview of the shape and progress of German idealism and romanticism, see the six essays in โThe Kantian Legacyโ in 
the Blackwell [3mCompanion to Continental Philosophy [0m. See also Steven Crowellโs essay on โNeo-Kantianismโ in the same volume. For a 
very useful overview of German romanticism and idealism and their relevance for contemporary philosophy, see the work of Andrew 
Bowie, especially [3mAesthetics and Subjectivity [0m(Manchester University Press, Manchester, 1990). John Stuart Millโs essays on 
Bentham and Coleridge, discussed in Chapter 3, can be found in [3mUtilitarianism and Other Essays [0m(Penguin, Harmondsworth, 1987). On 
the question of two cultures, see Stefan Colliniโs very helpful introduction to [3mThe Two Cultures [0m(Cambridge University Press, 
Cambridge, 1998). 

Chapter 4 begins by mentioning Rorty and Cavell. The best introduction to their work is their own writings; see Rortyโs now 
classic book, [3mPhilosophy and the Mirror of Nature [0m(Princeton University Press, Princeton, NJ, 1980) and Cavellโs wonderfully rich [3m
The Claim of Reason [0m(Oxford University Press, Oxford, 1979). On the question of tradition and on much else, see Husserlโs classic [3m
The Crisis of European Sciences and Transcendental Phenomenology [0m(Northwestern University Press, Evanston, 1970) and the 
โIntroductionโ to Heideggerโs [3mBeing and Time [0m(Blackwell, Oxford, 1962). 

Turning to Chapter 5, for a helpful discussion of nihilism before Nietzsche, see Michael Gillespieโs [3mNihilism Before Nietzsche [0m
(University of Chicago Press, Chicago, Ill., 1995). On nihilism in Nietzsche, see Mark Warren, [3mNietzsche and Political Thought [0m
(MIT Press, Cambridge, Mass., 1988) and Keith Ansell-Pearson, [3mNietzsche as a Political Thinker [0m(Cambridge University Press, 
Cambridge, 1994). For my own thoughts on how to respond to nihilism, see [3mVery Little โฆ Almost Nothing [0m(Routledge, London, 1997). 

For the Heidegger-Carnap controversy discussed in Chapter 6, Carnapโs essay can be found under the title โThe Elimination of 
Metaphysics through Logical Analysis of Languageโ, in [3mLogical Positivism [0m, ed. A. J. Ayer (Free Press, Glencoe, 1959). The most 
accurate translation of Heideggerโs โWhat is Metaphysics?โ can be found in [3mPathmarks [0m, ed. William McNeill (Cambridge University 
Press, Cambridge, 1998). The interesting โPostscriptโ and โIntroductionโ to โWhat is Metaphysics?โ can also be found in the same 
volume. The โYellow Brochureโ can be found in Otto Neurath, โThe Scientific Conception of the Worldโ (1929) in [3mEmpiricism and 
Sociology [0m(Reidel, Dordrecht, 1973). 

The argument of Chapter 7 on the problem of scientism and obscurantism was inspired by the work of Frank Cioffi: see his [3m
Wittgenstein on Freud and Frazer [0m(Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 1998). See also Habermas, [3mKnowledge and Human Interests [0m
(Polity Press, Cambridge, 1987) and Richard Bernstein, [3mBeyond Objectivism and Relativism [0m(University of Pennsylvania Press, 
Philadelphia, Pa., 1983). For the classic statement of the relation between causal explanation and interpretative understanding, 
see Peter Winch, [3mThe Idea of a Social Science and its Relation to Philosophy [0m(Routledge, London, 1990). 

On the notion of philosophy as conceptual creation alluded to in Chapter 8, see the opening chapters of Deleuze and Guattariโs 
wonderful [3mWhat is Philosophy? [0m(Columbia University Press, New York, 1994).
